I found the bug! Looking at the v38 migration code (lines 643-674):\n\n```typescript\nif (oldVersion < 38 && finalTargetVersion >= 38) {\n  panelUpgrades.push((panel: PanelModel) => {\n    if (panel.type === 'table' && panel.fieldConfig !== undefined) {\n      const displayMode = panel.fieldConfig.defaults?.custom?.displayMode;\n\n      // Update field configuration\n      if (displayMode !== undefined) {\n        // Migrate any options for the panel\n        panel.fieldConfig.defaults.custom.cellOptions = migrateTableDisplayModeToCellOptions(displayMode);\n\n        // Delete the legacy field\n        delete panel.fieldConfig.defaults.custom.displayMode;\n      }\n\n      // Update any overrides referencing the cell display mode\n      if (panel.fieldConfig?.overrides) {\n        for (const override of panel.fieldConfig.overrides) {\n          for (let j = 0; j < (override.properties?.length || 0); j++) {\n            let overrideDisplayMode = override.properties[j].value;\n            if (override.properties[j].id === 'custom.displayMode') {\n              override.properties[j].id = 'custom.cellOptions';\n              override.properties[j].value = migrateTableDisplayModeToCellOptions(overrideDisplayMode);\n            }\n          }\n        }\n      }\n    }\n\n    return panel;\n  });\n}\n```\n\nThe bug is on line 646: `if (panel.type === 'table' && panel.fieldConfig !== undefined) {`\n\nAnd specifically in line 650: `if (displayMode !== undefined) {`\n\nThe problem is that when a dashboard doesn't have explicit `fieldConfig.defaults.custom`, the code at line 652 tries to access `panel.fieldConfig.defaults.custom.cellOptions` without checking if `panel.fieldConfig.defaults.custom` actually exists!\n\n\nActually, wait—if `fieldConfig.defaults.custom` doesn't exist, the optional chaining on line 647 means `displayMode` will be undefined, so the condition on line 650 would skip the problematic assignment. But I need to trace through this more carefully. The real issue is that line 652 assumes `panel.fieldConfig.defaults.custom` exists when assigning `cellOptions`, but it could be null or undefined if only `fieldConfig` and `fieldConfig.defaults` exist without the `custom` object.\n\nLooking at the Go migration code in v38.go, the `processPanelsV38` function shows how the backend handles this: it checks if `defaults` exists, then if `custom` exists within it, before attempting to access or migrate `displayMode`. The Go code explicitly validates each level of nesting before proceeding, whereas the TypeScript code relies on optional chaining but doesn't properly guard the assignment when `custom` doesn't exist initially. So the optional chaining handles undefined cases properly. But the real issue is that line 646 only checks if `fieldConfig` exists, not whether `defaults` or `custom` are present. Even though the optional chaining prevents errors when accessing `displayMode`, the code then tries to assign to `panel.fieldConfig.defaults.custom.cellOptions` without verifying those nested properties exist first. This could cause a runtime error if `defaults` or `custom` is undefined. The migration also needs to call `migrateOverrides` to handle any field-level overrides that reference the display mode. The condition checking `displayMode !== undefined` guarantees the entire chain exists, so that's safe. But I'm noticing the overrides migration is supposed to run independently of whether defaults exist. Looking at the Go implementation, `migrateOverrides` handles cases where the structure might be incomplete, but the TypeScript version only processes overrides if they're present. The loop iterating through overrides needs to properly handle each override's field config structure. The defaults migration only runs when `displayMode !== undefined`, which means tables without an explicit display mode get skipped entirely even though they need `fieldConfig.defaults.custom` initialized. Additionally, if `fieldConfig.defaults` doesn't exist, line 652 would fail trying to set a property on undefined. The optional chaining on line 647 should protect against this, but there's a gap: what happens if a table has `fieldConfig` but lacks the nested structure needed for the migration to work properly?\n\nThe real issue emerges when overrides exist but defaults weren't migrated. If there's no `defaults.custom` because `displayMode` was undefined, the overrides migration still runs and tries to reference a property path that doesn't exist in the defaults. {"type":"assistant","message":{"model":"claude-haiku-4-5-20251001","id":"msg_01TD5Fum3aiuSjSZz754tPy1","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"thinking","thinking":"Looking at the test data, all the test cases have `fieldConfig.defaults.custom` with a `displayMode` field. So all these tests should pass.\n\nBut the description says \"Dashboards without explicit `fieldConfig.defaults.custom` lose column widths and text alignment\". This suggests that there are dashboards that DON'T have `fieldConfig.defaults.custom` at all.\n\nLet me search for what the actual issue description says. Looking back at the initial description:\n- \"A Grafana dashboard migration (v38) silently drops table panel field override configuration during import.\"\n- \"Dashboards without explicit `fieldConfig.defaults.custom` lose column widths and text alignment.\"\n\nSo the issue is with dashboards that DON'T have `fieldConfig.defaults.custom`. Let me look at the conditional logic more carefully.\n\nIn the frontend DashboardMigrator.ts (lines 646-673):\n```typescript\nif (panel.type === 'table' && panel.fieldConfig !== undefined) {\n  const displayMode = panel.fieldConfig.defaults?.custom?.displayMode;\n\n  // Update field configuration\n  if (displayMode !== undefined) {\n    // Migrate any options for the panel\n    panel.fieldConfig.defaults.custom.cellOptions = migrateTableDisplayModeToCellOptions(displayMode);\n\n    // Delete the legacy field\n    delete panel.fieldConfig.defaults.custom.displayMode;\n  }\n\n  // Update any overrides referencing the cell display mode\n  if (panel.fieldConfig?.overrides) {\n    for (const override of panel.fieldConfig.overrides) {\n      for (let j = 0; j < (override.properties?.length || 0); j++) {\n        let overrideDisplayMode = override.properties[j].value;\n        if (override.properties[j].id === 'custom.displayMode') {\n          override.properties[j].id = 'custom.cellOptions';\n          override.properties[j].value = migrateTableDisplayModeToCellOptions(overrideDisplayMode);\n\n\n```\n\nI see the problem now. When processing overrides, the code assigns `override.properties[j].value` directly to `overrideDisplayMode` without checking if it exists. Then it passes this potentially undefined value to `migrateTableDisplayModeToCellOptions()`, which can't handle undefined inputs. The Go migration code handles this case properly by checking for nil values before processing. The condition only checks if the property ID matches 'custom.displayMode', but doesn't verify that `override.properties[j].value` actually exists before attempting migration. If it's called with `undefined`, the switch statement would go to the `default` case and return `{ type: displayMode }`, which would be `{ type: undefined }`.\n\nNow, looking back at the DashboardMigrator.ts code (lines 660-669):\n\n```typescript\n// Update any overrides referencing the cell display mode\nif (panel.fieldConfig?.overrides) {\n  for (const override of panel.fieldConfig.overrides) {\n    for (let j = 0; j < (override.properties?.length || 0); j++) {\n      let overrideDisplayMode = override.properties[j].value;\n      if (override.properties[j].id === 'custom.displayMode') {\n        override.properties[j].id = 'custom.cellOptions';\n        override.properties[j].value = migrateTableDisplayModeToCellOptions(overrideDisplayMode);\n      }\n    }\n  }\n}\n```\n\nThe problem is clear now! When the value isn't a string, the Go code assigns an empty object instead, matching what the frontend does.\n\nThe real issue is the condition on line 646 checking only if `panel.fieldConfig` exists. This doesn't account for cases where `fieldConfig.defaults` or `fieldConfig.defaults.custom` might be undefined. The code assumes these nested properties are present whenever `displayMode !== undefined`, but overrides could exist independently of the defaults structure.
